Supply: Compromised Accounts</p><p class="MuiTypography-root MuiTypography-body1 ent-tss-gsvnz1-tab ent-css-1rblths">Scenario: How Cybercriminals Could Play Around with Compromised Accounts to Execute a Cyber Attack</p></div><div class="ent-tss-1fqoccs-showMore"><p class="MuiTypography-root MuiTypography-body1 ent-css-1rblths">Show More</p><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-tss-1jkhqog-svg-icon ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 17 11"><path d="M8.479,10.990 L8.479,10.990 C8.155,10.990 7.846,10.859 7.615,10.621 L0.347,3.127 C-0.128,2.636 -0.128,1.839 0.349,1.348 C0.825,0.857 1.598,0.857 2.074,1.348 L8.479,7.955 L14.887,1.348 C15.364,0.857 16.134,0.857 16.611,1.348 C17.087,1.839 17.087,2.636 16.611,3.127 L9.340,10.621 C9.112,10.859 8.802,10.990 8.479,10.990 Z"></path></svg></div></div></div><div class="ent-tss-g7g134-blogContentWrapper"><div class="ent-tss-o5kd66-enContentEditor en-content"><div id="ent-blog-content" class="ent-tss-16bdfpw-editable" data-slate-editor="true" data-slate-node="value" contenteditable="false" zindex="-1" style="position:relative;outline:none;white-space:pre-wrap;word-wrap:break-word"><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The gaming industry should really thank Covid-19: People are stuck at home, seeking indoor hobbies, and</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.theverge.com/2020/5/27/21270971/mobile-gaming-record-woodturning-ludo-king-coronavirus" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">giving online gaming a chance</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. With the rise of gamers and purchases, the online gaming industry is estimated</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.visualcapitalist.com/online-gaming-the-rise-of-a-multi-billion-dollar-industry/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">to reach $196 billion in revenue</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">by 2022. However, the growing success of this industry also calls attention to cybercriminals scouting out their new targets  and what better target could cybercriminals ask for than an industry thats up and coming and may not be prioritizing their security precautions as much as their industry advancement and profit. So, though this industry isnt valued at the trillions of dollars that the financial industry may be valued at, it still checks off boxes for two key factors that many profit-driven cyber criminals tend to seek: increase profits and minimize the complexity of the process in order to do so.</span></span></span></p><ul data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-1jjvw86-listDisc"><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">In order to assess the threat landscape of the gaming industry in light of Covid-19, we explored the risks that are potentially threatening employees and internal resources of the</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://newzoo.com/insights/rankings/top-25-companies-game-revenues/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">leaders of this industry</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">.</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.kelacyber.com/blog/darknet-threat-actors-are-not-playing-games-with-the-gaming-industry/#_ftn1" rel="follow" target="_self" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">[1]</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Weve included some of this blogs major key takeaways below:</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">KELA observed</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">multiple instances of supply and demand for initial network access of gaming companies</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">(especially their resources designed for developers).</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">KELA found nearly</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">1 million compromised accounts pertaining to gaming clients and employees, with 50% of them offered for sale during 2020.</span></strong></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">KELA detected</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">more than 500,000 leaked credentials</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">pertaining to employees of the leading companies in the gaming sector.</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The gaming industry is growing, in turn increasing the number of threats against it. By</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">proactively monitoring darknet communities</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">, organizations in this industry can</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">collect real-time valuable intelligence in order to help gain an external viewpoint on their organizations attack surfaces and mitigate cyber threats</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">.</span></span></span></li></ul><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="terms"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Terms</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Before diving into some threats targeting the gaming sector and the implications they may have, lets first review three commonly used terms throughout this blog post:</span></span></span></p><ul data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-1jjvw86-listDisc"><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Initial Network Access</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> A broad term referring to remote access to a computer in a compromised organization. Threat actors selling these accesses are referred to as</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://kelacyber.com/the-secret-life-of-an-initial-access-broker/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">initial access brokers</span></em></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> threat actors linking opportunistic campaigns with targeted attackers, namely ransomware operators.</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Compromised Accounts</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> Credentials, cookie sessions and additional technical fingerprints which are offered for sale on automated underground marketplaces such as</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://kelacyber.com/exploring-the-genesis-supply-chain-for-fun-and-profit/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Genesis</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">and more. These accounts are breached and stolen from victims computers generally via infections by banking trojans or other stealers. Such accounts can grant access to tools and software used in a targeted environment, such as RDP, VPN solutions, and more. They could be leveraged by a sophisticated actor to gain initial network access to the relevant corporates network.</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Leaked Credentials</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> Credentials from</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.zdnet.com/article/23600-hacked-databases-have-leaked-from-a-defunct-data-breach-index-site/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">various breached databases</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">constantly traded and circulating in the underground. Mostly, these databases include private and corporate email addresses and associated passwords, including plaintext ones. This data can enable attackers to access the companys resources and provide further malicious activity, such as account takeover attacks, social engineering, phishing and malware spreading campaigns.</span></span></span></li></ul><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">All these threats, altogether or separately, can be used in an attack chain aimed to compromise organizations.</span></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="supply-and-demand-threat-actors-specifically-looking-for-access-to-gaming-companies"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Supply and Demand: Threat Actors Specifically Looking for Access to Gaming Companies</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">For the past two months, weve observed several different actors looking for access to networks of gaming companies.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">A perfect example highlighting the demand for initial network access to gaming companies is seen in a listing by a Russian-speaking actor who was looking to purchase multiple types of accesses and databases. This actor specifically stated that he was interested in access to developers networks of Xbox (Microsoft), Nintendo, Qualcomm, and Apple.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" links</span></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Another example, from December 18, shows a threat actor selling data related to a major Japanese video game developer, which has</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.bleepingcomputer.com/news/security/koei-tecmo-discloses-data-breach-after-hacker-leaks-stolen-data/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">recently been disclosed as Koei Tecmo</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. The listing included FTP credentials  in this instance it does not necessarily indicate a network access, rather it provides an access point into the companys environment. The sample of data posted by the actor included email addresses, which KELA confirmed were indeed leaked from the companys server in March 2020 and later offered on</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.zdnet.com/article/23600-hacked-databases-have-leaked-from-a-defunct-data-breach-index-site/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Cit0day</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> a service that operated hacked databases and provided access to information for a subscription fee. Just a few days after the original listing, the threat actor decided to release all of Koei Tecmos data for free on the same forum that he published the original listing on.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Another instance showed a member of a Russian-speaking underground forum stating that he is ready to buy access to big gaming companies anywhere in the world. He claimed he was specifically interested in access to servers or repositories used for development.</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Such accesses, as well describe later, are frequently offered for sale in different underground markets.</span></strong></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" of online games in Latin America. Its possible that these are two unrelated events: the actor was looking for accesses to developers resources and separately worked on breaching the Latin American publisher whose access he soon offered for sale. However, it can also be true that he managed to buy some kind of access and transformed it into the server access offered for sale. Regardless of the connection of events, or the lack thereof, these two instances (as well as the others mentioned above) showcase the fact that threat actors are actively interested in targeting organizations in the gaming sector.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" unknown type to an online game of a German developer and access to a cloud storage solution (analogue of AWS) used by a major game developer.</span></span></span></p><div developer</span></em></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20224'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Access to cloud storage solution of a major game developer" title="Access to cloud storage solution of a major game developer" width="680" height="224" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x224/51024x336-1739810466922.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">Access to cloud storage solution of a major game developer</span></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">In private communication with the threat actor that offered access to the cloud storage solution, he claimed that the offer is no longer relevant, but alternatively offered something else  access to the network of a major Japanese game developer. Seeing that this was offered only in private conversation with the threat actor, we can assume that the number of threats is greater than actually presented.</span></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="supply-compromised-accounts"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">
Supply: Compromised Accounts</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Compromised accounts originate from infected computers (also known as</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">bots</span></em></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">), usually infected with banking trojans or infostealers. These bots are sold in automated shops, where new listings are added daily, essentially making it very easy for threat actors to attain access to a variety of resources. As one can imagine, a victims computer might have access to different services such as corporates portals, employees internal resources and platforms, social media accounts, kids school portals, bank accounts, and much more. These markets essentially assist threat actors to attain access to desired services with the click of a button and at a price of a couple of dollars per bot.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">KELA has been monitoring the major underground markets of this type for over 2.5 years and</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">has tracked nearly 1 million compromised accounts of employee- and client-facing resources of the 25 major gaming companies in question  with half of them being listed for sale in 2020 alone.</span></strong></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Its important to note that we detected compromised accounts to internal resources of nearly every company in question. These resources are meant to be used by employees, for example  Admin panels, VPNs, Jira instances, FTPs, SSOs, dev-related environments, and the list goes on and on.</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">As seen in the examples below, with a payment of just a couple of dollars a potential attacker can have access to the core areas of a companys network.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" company are being compromised.</span></em></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">VPN and SSO from Vidar, which might indicate some compromised internal resources of a leading gaming company.</span></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">For the past three months, weve observed four ransomware incidents impacting gaming companies  three of which were publicly reported. In addition, its possible that another major gaming developer was attacked, as Sodinokibi (REvil)</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://youtu.be/ZyQCQ1VZp8s?t=327" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">stated</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">in their interview.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Credentials to internal resources of recently attacked companies  such as VPN, website management portals, admin, Jira and more  were put up for sale and hence were available for any potential attacker prior to the cyberattacks that occurred.</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">We also detected an infected computer (bot) which had credential logs to plenty of sensitive accounts that could be accessed by attackers upon purchase: SSO, Kibana, Jira, adminconnect, service-now, Slack, VPN, password-manager and poweradmin of the company  all on a single bot  which strongly suggests that its used by an employee of the company with administrator rights.</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">This highly valuable bot was available for sale for less than $10</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Though we cannot directly correlate an attack on one of these victims to a bot that was listed in this market, this incident still highlights the risk that stands when an organizations sensitive resources are available for malicious use with an investment of just a couple of dollars.</span></strong></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="scenario-how-cybercriminals-could-play-around-with-compromised-accounts-to-execute-a-cyber-attack"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Scenario: How Cybercriminals Could Play Around with Compromised Accounts to Execute a Cyber Attack</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">To understand the risk, lets review a quick scenario of a possible c</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">yberattack that can lead to ransomware infection once a compromised account is offered on the underground automated shops</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">:</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Going back a few years, a threat actor would have to spend some time in the reconnaissance phase, carefully choosing victims and using multiple tools to get access to a valuable, functioning RDP server belonging to a corporation. Nowadays, an actor needs to only enter underground marketplaces and purchase a bot containing RDP credentials (another possible way  access one of the many</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://kelacyber.com/access-as-a-service-remote-access-markets-in-the-cybercrime-underground/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">remote access markets</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">and acquire multiple RDP corporate servers for a few hundred dollars).</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Next, the potential attacker needs to explore them and proceed with ones that look interesting  meaning they enable access to a network of a large company with significant revenue (and probably from a sector willing to pay ransom  government organizations, for instance, usually do not negotiate with ransomware operators). Finally, the actor will attempt to escalate privileges or install further tools in order to gain initial access.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">From that stage on, once theres initial access to a specific company, the attacker will usually choose one of two directions:</span></span></span></p><ul data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-1jjvw86-listDisc"><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Using the initial access to deploy ransomware on the companys network on their own.</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Selling it to</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://kelacyber.com/kelas-100-over-100-september2020-in-network-access-sales/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">ransomware affiliates</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">, who will deploy the ransomware themselves as part of a more organized crime.</span></span></span></li></ul><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">However, deploying ransomware is only one of the many different cyberattacks that these cybercriminals may attempt. This access could also enable them to initiate other offense such as corporate espionage, fraud, and other methods that could cause victims to incur severe financial losses.</span></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="supply-leaked-credentials"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Supply: Leaked Credentials</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">When looking at an exposure of a company, unfortunately, employees continue to remain as the main entry point, driving us to also analyze the sample companies leaked credentials stemming from 3rd party breaches. As of December 2020, weve</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">observed more than 500,000 leaked credentials pertaining to the employees of the 25 major gaming companies in question.</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">many cases, such credentials are available for free in the underground ecosystem, creating another opportunity for attackers to utilize for further attacks that could lead to profits.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">We found that these credentials also include high-profile email addresses such as senior employees and email addresses which are generally a significant channel in the company  invoice, purchasing, admin, HR-related emails, support and marketing are only some of the examples we noticed.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Its worth highlighting that KELAs caching capabilities allows visibility into additional context of leaked credentials, such as associated passwords to a certain email address, previous leaks of a specific email address and more. As part of our regular review, we unfortunately still come across a great deal of re-use of passwords, as can be examined in the example below:</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20291'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="a table with a list of passwords" title="re-use of passwords" width="680" height="291" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x291/91024x437-1739810636369.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x291/91024x437-1739810636369.png" alt="a table with a list of passwords" title="re-use of passwords" width="680" height="291"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">This single (censored) email address was leaked in numerous unique breaches, as well as some of the Collections dumps (referring here to Collection #1-5), and we can still see that once this user signs up with their corporate email address to a 3rd-party platform or website, they are most likely using an identical password across platforms. This exact behavior, which is unfortunately widely and commonly practiced, is really a human vulnerability that is continually being leveraged by threat actors, allowing attackers to gain access to services of interest.</span></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="scenario-leaked-credentials-are-level-1-in-the-attack-process"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Scenario: Leaked Credentials Are Level 1 in the Attack Process</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">As one of the main attack vectors still stands as phishing (also the vector</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://twitter.com/search?q=ubisoft%20from%3Amalwrhunterteam&amp;src=typed_query&amp;f=live" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">possibly used against Ubisoft</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">), theres room to exemplify how</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">leaked credentials</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">can be easily translated into a more significant attack.</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">Combolists</span></em></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">(email address: password lists) as well as databases with credentials originating from previous breaches are no news in the Darknet. Once an adversary is going through their reconnaissance phase of looking for their next potential target and puts their hands on an email address of interest, there are a variety of techniques that they may use. For instance:</span></span></span></p><ul data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-1jjvw86-listDisc"><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">An attacker can be seen using social engineering, or phishing attempts specifically tailored to the victim  either based on the place they work in, or on personal details. The aim is, of course, attaining the relevant credentials in order to gain access to services of interest, find an entry point to a targeted network and then escalate privileges and move laterally.</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">A threat actor can attempt to perform brute force and dictionary attacks, for which these databases with plain text passwords are highly useful. Once access was gained to a service of interest  the actor will continue to move laterally to eventually deploy ransomware, as we supposed earlier.</span></span></span></li></ul><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Hence, these databases may be highly useful for a potential attacker to perform a variety of attacks.</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The examples presented above reflect the types of threats KELA is detecting on a daily basis and can be used as an excellent example of why its crucial to educate employees and ensure that they understand the various ways that attackers may use to enter the organizations network.</span></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="training-the-targets-what-organizations-in-the-gaming-sector-should-do-to-reduce-cyber-threats"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Training the Targets: What Organizations in the Gaming Sector Should do to Reduce Cyber Threats</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The examples laid out in this blog present the ever-growing threats against the gaming sector that can be leverage by threat actors in a cyberattack. Over the years, new sectors will continue to emerge as the main targets for cyber criminals. These new targets are generally becoming popular among cyber criminals due to the simple fact that they are driving large sums of money. For that particular reason, well likely continue seeing new major targets rise, and organizations will need to prepare in accordance.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Organizations in the gaming sector have to act fast as they are the new target that cybercriminals are interested in. This preparation begins with security training to employees, including:</span></span></span></p><ol data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-pw0qn0-numberedList"><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Raising awareness to employees about the risks presented above.</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Enforcing password changes.</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Implementing unique password use and MFA policies.</span></span></span></li></ol><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The organizations in this sector will also be required to invest in different measures in order to ensure that they are protecting all of their different assets. Most importantly, these organizations should invest in ongoing monitoring of their assets, to get an external viewpoint of their organization as seen by cybercriminals. By monitoring mentions of their assets across the darknet, they will gain the necessary intelligence in order to help them better assess their exposure and prioritize security operations.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">As weve all been observing  attacks and attackers are becoming more sophisticated and customized to the victim. Some attackers try to search for the specific data and information that is relevant to the scope or industry of the victim and reproduce the successful attacks. As the gaming industry continues to grow in revenue, we will likely continue to detect more threats and attacks targeting the online gaming industry.</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">With constant monitoring of their assets exposure in the darknet, these organizations can proactively detect threats and map out their risk in order to foresee potential weaknesses in their environment.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.kelacyber.com/blog/darknet-threat-actors-are-not-playing-games-with-the-gaming-industry/#_ftnref1" rel="follow" target="_self" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">[1]</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Excluding Google, Apple and Microsoft. We have checked 53 domains which are related to the companies at issue, meaning not only looked into the companies main domains, but also some of their most popular games.</span></span></span></p></div></div></div></article><div class="ent-tss-1uoc0jk-popularQuestionsWrapper"><div class="ent-tss-165re9h-titleWrapper"><h2 class="MuiTypography-root MuiTypography-h2 !important"}}}},"defaultLocale":"en","host":"kelacyber.com","name":"kelacyber","section":"main","url":"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.kelacyber.com","appName":"kelacyber","widgetsWebsite":"kelacyber","website":"67583e08a0365cb3a8090712"},"home":{"userLoaded":false,"user":null,"showLoginDialog":false,"showLoginDialogBlocking":false,"userSubscribed":false,"loaded":false,"loadedKeepshoppers":false,"handleSubscribeClick":null,"recentQuestions":[],"comparisons":[],"blogPosts":[],"bottomBlogPosts":[],"featuredPosts":[],"productReviews":[],"targetedPosts":[],"top10Pages":[],"recommendedPosts":[],"products":[],"recentBlogPosts":[],"productComparisonCategories":[],"productComparisons":[],"productReviewCategories":[]},"ads":{"enabled":false,"toolbarStickyOffset":null,"fromTheBlogInFeed":{"desktop":{"slot":"","layoutKey":"","format":""},"mobile":{"slot":"","layoutKey":"","format":""}},"toolbarHeight":null,"client":null,"slot":{"list":null,"sticky":null,"square":null,"horizontal":null},"topic":{"mobile":{"format":null,"layoutKey":null,"slot":null},"desktop":{"format":null,"layoutKey":null,"slot":null}},"category":{"mobile":{"format":null,"layoutKey":null,"slot":null},"desktop":{"format":null,"layoutKey":null,"slot":null}},"productList":{"mobile":{"format":null,"layoutKey":null,"slot":null},"desktop":{"format":null,"layoutKey":null,"slot":null}}},"pages":{"pageData":null,"loaded":false},"learningCenter":{"post":null,"postLoaded":false},"forum":{"postLoaded":false,"post":null,"categoryLoaded":false,"category":null,"categoriesLoaded":false,"categories":[],"sideQuestions":[],"sideQuestionsLoaded":false,"tags":[],"tagsLoaded":false,"questionLoaded":false,"question":null,"questions":[],"questionsLoaded":false,"questionsPageSize":10,"questionsTotalPages":null,"totalQuestions":null,"mainPageCategorySlug":null,"mainPagePage":1,"popularTags":[]},"editorial":{"policy":null,"loadContributor":false,"writerBio":null,"contentPrinciples":null,"editorialTeam":null,"loadTeam":false,"bios":[]},"glossary":{"tags":[],"loaded":false,"popularTags":[]},"category":{"page":null,"pageLoaded":false},"topic":{"tag":null,"tagLoaded":false},"redirect":{"url":null,"name":null,"message":null,"redirectHttpCode":null},"page":{"post":{"previewImage":{"height":1080,"width":1920,"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fentail-assets.com\u002Fkelacyber\u002Fkela_18-1741678509919.jpg","alt":"a screenshot of a web page with a text description","title":"Darknet Threat Actors Are Not Playing Games with the Gaming Industry","caption":"a screenshot of a web page with a text description"},"previewVideo":null,"locale":"en","variant":"default","brief":null,"briefRichText":null,"showTOC":true,"showCoverImage":true,"title":"Darknet Threat Actors Are Not Playing Games with the Gaming Industry","richTitle":null,"subtitle":null,"richSubtitle":null,"text":"The gaming industry should really thank Covid-19: People are stuck at home, seeking indoor hobbies, andgiving online gaming a chance. With the rise of gamers and purchases, the online gaming industry is estimatedto reach $196 billion in revenueby 2022. However, the growing success of this industry also calls attention to cybercriminals scouting out their new targets  and what better target could cybercriminals ask for than an industry thats up and coming and may not be prioritizing their security precautions as much as their industry advancement and profit. So, though this industry isnt valued at the trillions of dollars that the financial industry may be valued at, it still checks off boxes for two key factors that many profit-driven cyber criminals tend to seek: increase profits and minimize the complexity of the process in order to do so.\nIn order to assess the threat landscape of the gaming industry in light of Covid-19, we explored the risks that are potentially threatening employees and internal resources of theleaders of this industry.[1]Weve included some of this blogs major key takeaways below:KELA observedmultiple instances of supply and demand for initial network access of gaming companies(especially their resources designed for developers).KELA found nearly1 million compromised accounts pertaining to gaming clients and employees, with 50% of them offered for sale during 2020.KELA detectedmore than 500,000 leaked credentialspertaining to employees of the leading companies in the gaming sector.The gaming industry is growing, in turn increasing the number of threats against it. Byproactively monitoring darknet communities, organizations in this industry cancollect real-time valuable intelligence in order to help gain an external viewpoint on their organizations attack surfaces and mitigate cyber threats.\nTerms\nBefore diving into some threats targeting the gaming sector and the implications they may have, lets first review three commonly used terms throughout this blog post:\nInitial Network Access A broad term referring to remote access to a computer in a compromised organization. Threat actors selling these accesses are referred to asinitial access brokers threat actors linking opportunistic campaigns with targeted attackers, namely ransomware operators.Compromised Accounts Credentials, cookie sessions and additional technical fingerprints which are offered for sale on automated underground marketplaces such asGenesisand more. These accounts are breached and stolen from victims computers generally via infections by banking trojans or other stealers. Such accounts can grant access to tools and software used in a targeted environment, such as RDP, VPN solutions, and more. They could be leveraged by a sophisticated actor to gain initial network access to the relevant corporates network.Leaked Credentials Credentials fromvarious breached databasesconstantly traded and circulating in the underground. Mostly, these databases include private and corporate email addresses and associated passwords, including plaintext ones. This data can enable attackers to access the companys resources and provide further malicious activity, such as account takeover attacks, social engineering, phishing and malware spreading campaigns.\nAll these threats, altogether or separately, can be used in an attack chain aimed to compromise organizations.\nSupply and Demand: Threat Actors Specifically Looking for Access to Gaming Companies\nFor the past two months, weve observed several different actors looking for access to networks of gaming companies.\nA perfect example highlighting the demand for initial network access to gaming companies is seen in a listing by a Russian-speaking actor who was looking to purchase multiple types of accesses and databases. This actor specifically stated that he was interested in access to developers networks of Xbox (Microsoft), Nintendo, Qualcomm, and Apple.\n\nA threat actor stating he is interested in access to developers resources of gaming companies in a format developer.COMPANY.com or similar links\nAnother example, from December 18, shows a threat actor selling data related to a major Japanese video game developer, which hasrecently been disclosed as Koei Tecmo. The listing included FTP credentials  in this instance it does not necessarily indicate a network access, rather it provides an access point into the companys environment. The sample of data posted by the actor included email addresses, which KELA confirmed were indeed leaked from the companys server in March 2020 and later offered onCit0day a service that operated hacked databases and provided access to information for a subscription fee. Just a few days after the original listing, the threat actor decided to release all of Koei Tecmos data for free on the same forum that he published the original listing on.\nAnother instance showed a member of a Russian-speaking underground forum stating that he is ready to buy access to big gaming companies anywhere in the world. He claimed he was specifically interested in access to servers or repositories used for development.Such accesses, as well describe later, are frequently offered for sale in different underground markets.\n\nA threat actor seeking for access to developments servers to GitHub analogues where they keep their code of gaming companies\nInterestingly, four days later the same actor offered access to a server of a publisher of online games in Latin America. Its possible that these are two unrelated events: the actor was looking for accesses to developers resources and separately worked on breaching the Latin American publisher whose access he soon offered for sale. However, it can also be true that he managed to buy some kind of access and transformed it into the server access offered for sale. Regardless of the connection of events, or the lack thereof, these two instances (as well as the others mentioned above) showcase the fact that threat actors are actively interested in targeting organizations in the gaming sector.\n\nThe threat actor, previously looking to buy access to gaming companies, is himself offering access to a gaming companys server\nSome other offers that we observed by initial access brokers include access of an unknown type to an online game of a German developer and access to a cloud storage solution (analogue of AWS) used by a major game developer.\n\nAccess to an online game of a Germany-based developer\n\nAccess to cloud storage solution of a major game developer\nIn private communication with the threat actor that offered access to the cloud storage solution, he claimed that the offer is no longer relevant, but alternatively offered something else  access to the network of a major Japanese game developer. Seeing that this was offered only in private conversation with the threat actor, we can assume that the number of threats is greater than actually presented.\n\nSupply: Compromised Accounts\nCompromised accounts originate from infected computers (also known asbots), usually infected with banking trojans or infostealers. These bots are sold in automated shops, where new listings are added daily, essentially making it very easy for threat actors to attain access to a variety of resources. As one can imagine, a victims computer might have access to different services such as corporates portals, employees internal resources and platforms, social media accounts, kids school portals, bank accounts, and much more. These markets essentially assist threat actors to attain access to desired services with the click of a button and at a price of a couple of dollars per bot.\nKELA has been monitoring the major underground markets of this type for over 2.5 years andhas tracked nearly 1 million compromised accounts of employee- and client-facing resources of the 25 major gaming companies in question  with half of them being listed for sale in 2020 alone.\nIts important to note that we detected compromised accounts to internal resources of nearly every company in question. These resources are meant to be used by employees, for example  Admin panels, VPNs, Jira instances, FTPs, SSOs, dev-related environments, and the list goes on and on.As seen in the examples below, with a payment of just a couple of dollars a potential attacker can have access to the core areas of a companys network.\n\nDeveloper-related and Jira credentials collected from the information stealer Vidar, which could indicate some internal resources of a leading gaming company are being compromised\n\nAdmin-related credentials collected from the information stealer AZORult, which might indicate some internal resources of a leading gaming company are being compromised.\n\nVPN and SSO from Vidar, which might indicate some compromised internal resources of a leading gaming company.\nFor the past three months, weve observed four ransomware incidents impacting gaming companies  three of which were publicly reported. In addition, its possible that another major gaming developer was attacked, as Sodinokibi (REvil)statedin their interview.\nCredentials to internal resources of recently attacked companies  such as VPN, website management portals, admin, Jira and more  were put up for sale and hence were available for any potential attacker prior to the cyberattacks that occurred.We also detected an infected computer (bot) which had credential logs to plenty of sensitive accounts that could be accessed by attackers upon purchase: SSO, Kibana, Jira, adminconnect, service-now, Slack, VPN, password-manager and poweradmin of the company  all on a single bot  which strongly suggests that its used by an employee of the company with administrator rights.This highly valuable bot was available for sale for less than $10.\nThough we cannot directly correlate an attack on one of these victims to a bot that was listed in this market, this incident still highlights the risk that stands when an organizations sensitive resources are available for malicious use with an investment of just a couple of dollars.\nScenario: How Cybercriminals Could Play Around with Compromised Accounts to Execute a Cyber Attack\nTo understand the risk, lets review a quick scenario of a possible cyberattack that can lead to ransomware infection once a compromised account is offered on the underground automated shops:\nGoing back a few years, a threat actor would have to spend some time in the reconnaissance phase, carefully choosing victims and using multiple tools to get access to a valuable, functioning RDP server belonging to a corporation. Nowadays, an actor needs to only enter underground marketplaces and purchase a bot containing RDP credentials (another possible way  access one of the manyremote access marketsand acquire multiple RDP corporate servers for a few hundred dollars).\nNext, the potential attacker needs to explore them and proceed with ones that look interesting  meaning they enable access to a network of a large company with significant revenue (and probably from a sector willing to pay ransom  government organizations, for instance, usually do not negotiate with ransomware operators). Finally, the actor will attempt to escalate privileges or install further tools in order to gain initial access.\nFrom that stage on, once theres initial access to a specific company, the attacker will usually choose one of two directions:\nUsing the initial access to deploy ransomware on the companys network on their own.Selling it toransomware affiliates, who will deploy the ransomware themselves as part of a more organized crime.\nHowever, deploying ransomware is only one of the many different cyberattacks that these cybercriminals may attempt. This access could also enable them to initiate other offense such as corporate espionage, fraud, and other methods that could cause victims to incur severe financial losses.\nSupply: Leaked Credentials\nWhen looking at an exposure of a company, unfortunately, employees continue to remain as the main entry point, driving us to also analyze the sample companies leaked credentials stemming from 3rd party breaches. As of December 2020, weveobserved more than 500,000 leaked credentials pertaining to the employees of the 25 major gaming companies in question.many cases, such credentials are available for free in the underground ecosystem, creating another opportunity for attackers to utilize for further attacks that could lead to profits.\nWe found that these credentials also include high-profile email addresses such as senior employees and email addresses which are generally a significant channel in the company  invoice, purchasing, admin, HR-related emails, support and marketing are only some of the examples we noticed.\nIts worth highlighting that KELAs caching capabilities allows visibility into additional context of leaked credentials, such as associated passwords to a certain email address, previous leaks of a specific email address and more. As part of our regular review, we unfortunately still come across a great deal of re-use of passwords, as can be examined in the example below:\n\nThis single (censored) email address was leaked in numerous unique breaches, as well as some of the Collections dumps (referring here to Collection #1-5), and we can still see that once this user signs up with their corporate email address to a 3rd-party platform or website, they are most likely using an identical password across platforms. This exact behavior, which is unfortunately widely and commonly practiced, is really a human vulnerability that is continually being leveraged by threat actors, allowing attackers to gain access to services of interest.\nScenario: Leaked Credentials Are Level 1 in the Attack Process\nAs one of the main attack vectors still stands as phishing (also the vectorpossibly used against Ubisoft), theres room to exemplify howleaked credentialscan be easily translated into a more significant attack.Combolists(email address: password lists) as well as databases with credentials originating from previous breaches are no news in the Darknet. Once an adversary is going through their reconnaissance phase of looking for their next potential target and puts their hands on an email address of interest, there are a variety of techniques that they may use. For instance:\nAn attacker can be seen using social engineering, or phishing attempts specifically tailored to the victim  either based on the place they work in, or on personal details. The aim is, of course, attaining the relevant credentials in order to gain access to services of interest, find an entry point to a targeted network and then escalate privileges and move laterally.A threat actor can attempt to perform brute force and dictionary attacks, for which these databases with plain text passwords are highly useful. Once access was gained to a service of interest  the actor will continue to move laterally to eventually deploy ransomware, as we supposed earlier.\nHence, these databases may be highly useful for a potential attacker to perform a variety of attacks.The examples presented above reflect the types of threats KELA is detecting on a daily basis and can be used as an excellent example of why its crucial to educate employees and ensure that they understand the various ways that attackers may use to enter the organizations network.\nTraining the Targets: What Organizations in the Gaming Sector Should do to Reduce Cyber Threats\nThe examples laid out in this blog present the ever-growing threats against the gaming sector that can be leverage by threat actors in a cyberattack. Over the years, new sectors will continue to emerge as the main targets for cyber criminals. These new targets are generally becoming popular among cyber criminals due to the simple fact that they are driving large sums of money. For that particular reason, well likely continue seeing new major targets rise, and organizations will need to prepare in accordance.\nOrganizations in the gaming sector have to act fast as they are the new target that cybercriminals are interested in. This preparation begins with security training to employees, including:\nRaising awareness to employees about the risks presented above.Enforcing password changes.Implementing unique password use and MFA policies.\nThe organizations in this sector will also be required to invest in different measures in order to ensure that they are protecting all of their different assets. Most importantly, these organizations should invest in ongoing monitoring of their assets, to get an external viewpoint of their organization as seen by cybercriminals. By monitoring mentions of their assets across the darknet, they will gain the necessary intelligence in order to help them better assess their exposure and prioritize security operations.\nAs weve all been observing  attacks and attackers are becoming more sophisticated and customized to the victim. Some attackers try to search for the specific data and information that is relevant to the scope or industry of the victim and reproduce the successful attacks. As the gaming industry continues to grow in revenue, we will likely continue to detect more threats and attacks targeting the online gaming industry.With constant monitoring of their assets exposure in the darknet, these organizations can proactively detect threats and map out their risk in order to foresee potential weaknesses in their environment.\n[1]Excluding Google, Apple and Microsoft. We have checked 53 domains which are related to the companies at issue, meaning not only looked into the companies main domains, but also some of their most popular games.","richText":"[{\"type\":\"paragraph\",\"children\":[{\"text\":\"The gaming industry should really thank Covid-19: People are stuck at home, seeking indoor hobbies, and\"},{\"type\":\"link\",\"url\":\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.theverge.com\u002F2020\u002F5\u002F27\u002F21270971\u002Fmobile-gaming-record-woodturning-ludo-king-coronavirus\",\"newTab\":true,\"rel\":\"nofollow\",\"children\":[{\"text\":\"giving online gaming a chance\"}]},{\"text\":\".             <script>

        // if getParameterByName function is not defined, define it
        if (typeof getParameterByName === 'undefined') {
            function getParameterByName(name, url) {
                if (!url) url = window.location.href;
                name = name.replace(/[\[\]]/g, "\\$&");
                var regex = new RegExp("[?&]" + name + "(=([^&#]*)|&|#|$)"),
                    results = regex.exec(url);
                if (!results) return null;
                if (!results[2]) return '';
                return decodeURIComponent(results[2].replace(/\+/g, " "));
            }
        }

        jQuery(document).ready(function($) {

            // First detect if _GET params are present.
            // We only need utm_source, utm_medium and utm_campaign
            var utm_source = getParameterByName('utm_source');
            var utm_medium = getParameterByName('utm_medium');
            var utm_campaign = getParameterByName('utm_campaign');
            var utm_content = getParameterByName('utm_content');
            var utm_term = getParameterByName('utm_term');

            // Next we will use Cookies.js to set cookies

            // If _GET params are present, set cookies
            if (utm_source) {
                Cookies.set('kela_utm_source', utm_source, { expires: 1, path: '/' });
            }
            if (utm_medium) {
                Cookies.set('kela_utm_medium', utm_medium, { expires: 1, path: '/' });
            }
            if (utm_campaign) {
                Cookies.set('kela_utm_campaign', utm_campaign, { expires: 1, path: '/' });
            }
            if (utm_content) {
                Cookies.set('kela_utm_content', utm_content, { expires: 1, path: '/' });
            }
            if (utm_term) {
                Cookies.set('kela_utm_term', utm_term, { expires: 1, path: '/' });
            }

            // Check if cookies are present
            if (Cookies.get('kela_utm_source')) {
                utm_source = Cookies.get('kela_utm_source');
            }
            if (Cookies.get('kela_utm_medium')) {
                utm_medium = Cookies.get('kela_utm_medium');
            }
            if (Cookies.get('kela_utm_campaign')) {
                utm_campaign = Cookies.get('kela_utm_campaign');
            }
            if (Cookies.get('kela_utm_content')) {
                utm_content = Cookies.get('kela_utm_content');
            }
            if (Cookies.get('kela_utm_term')) {
                utm_term = Cookies.get('kela_utm_term');
            }

            // Wait 4 seconds for the form to load
            setTimeout(function() {
                
                // Find if hidden fields exist, for example: .hs-utm_campaign input
                if ($(".hs-utm_source input").length) {
                    $(".hs-utm_source input").val(utm_source);
                }
                if ($(".hs-utm_medium input").length) {
                    $(".hs-utm_medium input").val(utm_medium);
                }   

                if ($(".hs-utm_campaign input").length) {
                    $(".hs-utm_campaign input").val(utm_campaign);
                }

                if ($(".hs-utm_content input").length) {
                    $(".hs-utm_content input").val(utm_content);
                }

                if ($(".hs-utm_term input").length) {
                    $(".hs-utm_term input").val(utm_term);
                }

                if ($(".gfield--type-text.utm_source input").length) {
                    $(".gfield--type-text.utm_source input").val(utm_source);
                }

                if ($(".gfield--type-text.utm_medium input").length) {
                    $(".gfield--type-text.utm_medium input").val(utm_medium);
                }

                if ($(".gfield--type-text.utm_campaign input").length) {
                    $(".gfield--type-text.utm_campaign input").val(utm_campaign);
                }

                if ($(".gfield--type-text.utm_content input").length) {
                    $(".gfield--type-text.utm_content input").val(utm_content);
                }

                if ($(".gfield--type-text.utm_term input").length) {
                    $(".gfield--type-text.utm_term input").val(utm_term);
                }
                
            }, 4000);

            
        });
    </script>
